<div id="review_522926469" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/522926469" />
    <a title="Christopher" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7919191-christopher"><img alt="Christopher" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1512953930p2/7919191.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/522926469?book_show_action=true">Jan 31, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Christopher" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Christopher" href="/user/show/7919191-christopher">Christopher</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer522926469&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer522926469" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10399682520410406189">The Yearling is not a book for those who get bored easily. The book is slow-moving, taking its time to vividly describe the Florida wild. Although the description is indeed colorful and paints the picture well, there's a fine line to walk between enough description, and too much. Sadly, The Yearling doesn't quite walk this line. <br/>	The book is the tale of young Jody Baxter, a twelve year old boy living in the wild of Florida. When his father shoots a doe, Jody convinces him to let him take the you</span>
  <span id="freeText10399682520410406189" style="display:none">The Yearling is not a book for those who get bored easily. The book is slow-moving, taking its time to vividly describe the Florida wild. Although the description is indeed colorful and paints the picture well, there's a fine line to walk between enough description, and too much. Sadly, The Yearling doesn't quite walk this line. <br />	The book is the tale of young Jody Baxter, a twelve year old boy living in the wild of Florida. When his father shoots a doe, Jody convinces him to let him take the young faun into his care. Jody names the faun Flag, and a bond develops between the two. However, Jody's mother accidentally shoots Flag, fatally wounding him. Convinced his mother did it on purpose, Jody runs away in anger. After he finally returns, his father tells him that he is now a man, and Jody realizes that Flag wasn't the only thing that died. <br />	Marjorie Kinnan Rawlings certainly knows how to write good, fully-fledged characters. When Penny is bitten, the readers afraid for him. When Jody gets to keep the faun, the readers are happy for him. The book convincingly takes one on a journey through this young boy's life as he grows up and faces the reality of death and loss. Although the ending did seem a bit rushed, perhaps losing some of the potential emotion, it was a decent end. The issue at the heart of this book, however, is not the characters. <br />	The issue with this book is two-fold. First, is the over-descriptiveness of the prose. Second, the resulting slow-moving plot. <br />	A book is a package, created to either entertain, or to share a theme. Both are valid options, and some books combine the two. Imagine that one ordered a fragile glass plate from Amazon. The package was shipped, but when one opens it, they find the plate broken. Upon examination, it's discovered that there was no packaging to cushion the plate. Imagine now, that the order shipped, but arrived without a box. The plate was wrapped in styrofoam. Still, the plate would probably be broken, and the customer disappointed. Imagine once more that the order arrived in a nice, brown box. Packaging was present within in an abundance, but there was no plate. Still the customer would be disappointed. But why?<br />	The customer would be disappointed because he paid for a full package. He paid for the plate, and for the plate's shipping. A book works much the same way. The best books are packages with everything present: the box, the packaging, and the item itself. All of it has to be there, each depending on the next for their weight and significance. Prose is the box, packaging is the plot and characters, and the item is the theme. Too much prose with too little plot, characters, and theme ends in a dull book. Too little plot or character, even if the theme and prose is fantastic, is still a dull book. Everything has to be there in it's proper proportions to deliver a really good book. <br />	Sadly, The Yearling has a box that's a bit too big, and ends up eliminating the point of the packaging and the item itself. The prose is too flowery, and too overbearing. Rawlings is good at description but only so much is needed per book. A writer has to use color carefully. The focus shouldn't be on topping Van Gogh, the focus should be on providing enough color to illuminate the scene, but not so much that the scene is drowned out in favor of the color. The Yearling, makes this mistake. Rawlings tries to be Van Gogh, lavishly taking her time belaboring sprawling trees and hanging moss, without giving us the reasons why we should care about the trees and the moss. <br />	The characters, plot, and theme were interesting, but they were lost in the whirlpool of description, and more description, and even more description. Using simple words, the primary colors if you will, to paint a picture is fantastic. There's no need to give us an art class though. Big, long, extravagant words should be used sparingly. As a wise man once said, "Never use a big word where a diminutive one will do."</span>
  <a data-text-id="10399682520410406189" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2878971014" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2878971014" />
    <a title="Judy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22241323-judy"><img alt="Judy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595043210p2/22241323.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2878971014?book_show_action=true">Jul 01, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Judy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Judy" href="/user/show/22241323-judy">Judy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/3760541-the-yearling">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22241323-judy?shelf=sci-env-natlhx">sci-env-natlhx</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22241323-judy?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22241323-judy?shelf=award-pulitzer">award-pulitzer</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22241323-judy?shelf=us-florida-f-nf">us-florida-f-nf</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2878971014" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18069169881307293668"><b>Childhood memories:</b><br/>I've avoided reading this book for decades. I watched the movie once when I was quite young and was horrified. Bambi was sad, but this was devastating.<br/><br/>Recently, an older friend gave me her first-edition copy of the book, knowing that I'd value it, but not realizing the depth of my feelings about the story. She, however, convinced me that I should give it a try since I enjoyed Rawling's <u>Cross Creek</u>.<br/><br/><b>2019 review</b><br/>From 0 stars to 5 stars. Obviously, my thinking has changed over the </span>
  <span id="freeText18069169881307293668" style="display:none"><b>Childhood memories:</b><br />I've avoided reading this book for decades. I watched the movie once when I was quite young and was horrified. Bambi was sad, but this was devastating.<br /><br />Recently, an older friend gave me her first-edition copy of the book, knowing that I'd value it, but not realizing the depth of my feelings about the story. She, however, convinced me that I should give it a try since I enjoyed Rawling's <u>Cross Creek</u>.<br /><br /><b>2019 review</b><br />From 0 stars to 5 stars. Obviously, my thinking has changed over the years. And, it's highly probable that I steeled myself for the inevitable so it wouldn't be as painful. As a child, I would have identified with the deer, whereas now I understand the adults' point of view and I can sympathize with Jody.<br /><br />While this is often described as a story of a boy and his fawn, it actually depicts a boy's coming-of age, the relationship of a boy and his father, and life in the wilds of Florida in the 1870s. At first, I was impatient with all of the description, mainly because I can't visualize the plants and birds that Rawlings mentions. Ideally, this book would be illustrated so the reader can get some idea of the setting. I often turn to the internet for answers, but if I'd done that here, it would have taken far too long to read each page.<br /><br />How closely did the 1946 film follow the novel? Rawlings writes so much about setting, backstory, and inner thoughts, that I'd expect quite a bit of difference, starting with the casting. Jane Wyman and Gregory Peck don't fit my image of Ma and Pa Baxter. And the nudity? I suspect that was omitted. When re-watching the movie, I'll pay close attention to the setting. Apparently it was filmed in the location that Rawlings said she had in mind while she was writing.<br /><br />I suspect that these quotes reflect Rawlings' own values.<br /><br />p 18, Pa Baxter's thoughts about his land:<br /><i>The clearing was peculiarly his own. The wild animals seemed less predatory to him than people he had known. The forays of bear and wolf and wild-cat and panther on stock were understandable, which was more than he could say of human cruelties.</i><br /><br />p 19, a little geological history:<br /><i>A scarcity of water was the only draw-back to the location. ... The sink-hole was a phenomenon common to the Florida limestone regions. ... The sinkhole included with Penny Baxter's land contained, unfortunately, no flowing spring. But a pure filtered water seeped day and night through the high banks and formed a pool at the bottom.</i><br /><br />p 34, about hunting:<br /><i>"Son, I've helt back my shot and contented myself with watching' many a times when creators was feedin' harmless and innocent. It goes agin me to crack down at such a time. Or when creators is matin'. Now and agin when it was git meat or the Baxters go hungry, I've done what I've no likin' to do. And don't you grow up like the Forresters, killin' meat you got no use for, for the fun of it.</i><br /><br />p 201, a peek at Jody's thoughts:<br /><i>It was good to become old and see the sights and hear the sounds that men saw and heard, like Buck [Forrester] and his father. That was why he liked to lie flat on his belly on the floor, or on the earth before the camp-fire, while men talked. They had seen marvels, and the older they were, the more marvels they had seen.</i><br /><br />That last quote got me thinking about how times have changed. Kids used to listen to their elders and while doing so they heard stories about not only experiences but about their own families and local landmarks. My guess is that most kids ignore the 'oldsters,' and prefer their electronic gadgets to sitting at the knees of their grandparents. That's sad for the grandparents and the kids are missing out on the opportunity to learn first-hand about an earlier generation.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18069169881307293668" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_172217197" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/172217197" />
    <a title="Cee" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4472055-cee"><img alt="Cee" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307839471p2/4472055.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/172217197?book_show_action=true">May 30, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cee" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cee" href="/user/show/4472055-cee">Cee</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer172217197" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14491586299034335586">Looking at the book cover, I thought I was going to read about a child and his pet deer. Well, there is a child and there is a deer but their relationship is only secondary to that of the boy and his father. I think that this is foremost a story about a father and his son and the valuable life lessons that he gives his child through one memorable year. Taking care of a deer is just one of those life lessons. <br/><br/>The boy Jody lives with his parents in the Florida brush in a time when children were ex</span>
  <span id="freeText14491586299034335586" style="display:none">Looking at the book cover, I thought I was going to read about a child and his pet deer. Well, there is a child and there is a deer but their relationship is only secondary to that of the boy and his father. I think that this is foremost a story about a father and his son and the valuable life lessons that he gives his child through one memorable year. Taking care of a deer is just one of those life lessons. <br /><br />The boy Jody lives with his parents in the Florida brush in a time when children were expected to hoe and weed the fields, fetch water and chuck wood as soon as they can walk and talk. But Penny Baxter, Jody's father, wants his son to enjoy his childhood as long as he can. He says, "Leave him kick up his heels and run away. Leave him build his flutter-mills. The day'll come, he'll not even care to."<br /><br />And so, Jody has a magical year. It is a year that includes a bear hunt, a rattlesnake bite, a flood, and yes, a pet deer. A lot of times Penny's leniency with Jody frustrates Ma Baxter, a stern yet responsible woman who takes care of her men while living in the rough. After this singular year they are all changed and Jody has shed childhood without being forced to.<br /><br />I love the wisdom of Penny Baxter, Jody's father. In fact, I love everything about him. In short, he is an ideal dad. Perhaps it is even too good to be true but it works out in this book. I love the thought of a beautiful father and son relationship and where the son learns what is right and wrong from his own dad.<br /><br />There are a lot of descriptives in this book, scenery and food among others, that plant me firmly in the time period. I don't mind reading what flowers are blooming or what animals are doing at the spring. I love the imagery. There is an abundance food mentioned in the book, the made from scratch type of food that left me hungry and wanting to cook.<br /><br />This kind of book is generally categorized as YA or children's literature. Nevertheless, adults can enjoy reading it while remembering their own coming of age stories and reflecting on the nuggets of wisdom written throughout the story. A	Pulitzer prize winner, The Yearling is most deserving of this accolade.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14491586299034335586" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/63827421?book_show_action=true">Jul 17, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kathleen L. L." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kathleen L. L." href="/user/show/2531698-kathleen-l-l">Kathleen L. L.</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer63827421&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer63827421" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6826734952176369274">By today's standards, this slow-paced and ponderous work seems ill-fitting to Y/A, and yet too ordinary or naive for adult readerships. But to me in fifth grade, perhaps because of my thirst for another time and place from my poverty and fear, and of course my love of animals, this was the perfect fit, if a bit haunting. <br/>I'll never forget Jodie's bittersweet affection for Flag. Knowing to keep his family alive from starvation meant to kill his beloved pet, he chooses what a man would do, though </span>
  <span id="freeText6826734952176369274" style="display:none">By today's standards, this slow-paced and ponderous work seems ill-fitting to Y/A, and yet too ordinary or naive for adult readerships. But to me in fifth grade, perhaps because of my thirst for another time and place from my poverty and fear, and of course my love of animals, this was the perfect fit, if a bit haunting. <br />I'll never forget Jodie's bittersweet affection for Flag. Knowing to keep his family alive from starvation meant to kill his beloved pet, he chooses what a man would do, though still a boy himself. His lonely life in the Everglades, surviving snake bite, floods, rogue bears and the rough life of a homesteader seemed the perfect metaphors to my life of modern trials in government housing, trying to learn how to navigate through rough neighborhoods, street-smart kids, urban blight, and fatherlessness. Rawlings allowed me to both escape and yet experience sympathy through her tender rendering of timeless portraits of a child and his pet--unconditional love that could never be properly requited.<br />This is a lost classic which may never receive the recognition it deserves.</span>

<div id="review_4638122" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/4638122" />
    <a title="Jenn Garrett" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/257020-jenn-garrett"><img alt="Jenn Garrett" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1187281118p2/257020.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/4638122?book_show_action=true">Aug 16, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jenn Garrett" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jenn Garrett" href="/user/show/257020-jenn-garrett">Jenn Garrett</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer4638122" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2054929869272658673">I had never read this classic, despite the fact that it is set just south of here, I have seen the original manuscript at UF archives, and been to Rawlings house where whe wrote it. It was slow, but there is some nice vivid imagery of rural Florida. The story is centered around a boy, maybe ten years old, and his expireinces growing up as an early settler in Florida. I was shocked by Rawlings descriptions of some of the female characters, but understood after finishing the book whay she wrote th</span>
  <span id="freeText2054929869272658673" style="display:none">I had never read this classic, despite the fact that it is set just south of here, I have seen the original manuscript at UF archives, and been to Rawlings house where whe wrote it. It was slow, but there is some nice vivid imagery of rural Florida. The story is centered around a boy, maybe ten years old, and his expireinces growing up as an early settler in Florida. I was shocked by Rawlings descriptions of some of the female characters, but understood after finishing the book whay she wrote them as she did. Despite the slow going, the book deserves its praise- a beautiful metaphor for growing up. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2054929869272658673" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1964212873" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1964212873" />
    <a title="Luisa Knight" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/66567613-luisa-knight"><img alt="Luisa Knight" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1491521288p2/66567613.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1964212873?book_show_action=true">Apr 06, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Luisa Knight" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Luisa Knight" href="/user/show/66567613-luisa-knight">Luisa Knight</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780020449317" href="/book/show/863798.The_Yearling">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/66567613-luisa-knight?shelf=family-read-aloud">family-read-aloud</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/66567613-luisa-knight?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/66567613-luisa-knight?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/66567613-luisa-knight?shelf=honey-for-a-childs-heart">honey-for-a-childs-heart</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1964212873" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4123245184124509193">I am not one for animal stories ... but this was so well told and more about a father and son's beautiful relationship than a pet. Lush descriptions, superb character development and hints of Gene Stratton-Porter-ness make this a captivating read.<br/><br/>Cleanliness: <br/><br/>Profanity<br/>Mild Obscenities & Substitutions - 25 Incidents: blasted, tarnation, h*ll (place), c*ck (rooster), h*ll, dad-ratted, boogers’ll, boogerish, d*mn<br/>Religious Profanities - 21 Incidents: Oh dear goodness, Gawd, by God, bless Heaven, mer</span>
  <span id="freeText4123245184124509193" style="display:none">I am not one for animal stories ... but this was so well told and more about a father and son's beautiful relationship than a pet. Lush descriptions, superb character development and hints of Gene Stratton-Porter-ness make this a captivating read.<br /><br />Cleanliness: <br /><br />Profanity<br />Mild Obscenities &amp; Substitutions - 25 Incidents: blasted, tarnation, h*ll (place), c*ck (rooster), h*ll, dad-ratted, boogers’ll, boogerish, d*mn<br />Religious Profanities - 21 Incidents: Oh dear goodness, Gawd, by God, bless Heaven, merciful Heaven, Lord save us, for the Lord's sake, Lord, thank the Lord<br />Derogatory Terms - 1 Incident: b*tch (referring to a dog)<br /><br />Sexual Content<br />Miscellaneous - 35 Incidents: “Soft gray breast of a mourning dove.” Recalls how a man married a buxom woman. Mentions how a man planned to have plenty of children. His wife was built for it but his “seed were as puny as himself.” Mentions the “teat” of an animal. Mentions the breast of a dog. A group of naked brothers sing and dance and get drunk together (non-descriptive and referenced a few times). A man mentions he has a sweetheart and how he’d sing and dance with her. The girl, however, is tagging two men along because it is brought up that she is another man’s sweetheart. A boy had slept naked with a bear skin for cover. The word “sex” is used to mean gender. A father teases his son about liking a girl (he does not like her and it is brought up again along with another man’s girlfriend). An old woman’s “plump breasts quivered.” “The closed door concealed all manner of things behind its black bosom.” In his excitement, a boy runs out of his room naked to greet an old friend. A boy takes off his clothes in a rain storm. The words “teats” and “udders” are used when describing milking a cow. There is a discussion on a girl who is courting two guys at once. A man works without his shirt, his chest is described. Mentions an animal’s breasts and her young feeding. A man and a boy “were stripped to their breeches.” A man takes off his breeches to warm by the fire. Mentions how a girl finally made up her mind about which guy she liked and got on a boat to follow him. Referring to a twelve year old son and his mother: “He went to her and smelled of her and rubbed his nose against her flannel breast.” Describing a hug. “I got a gal in Jacksonville … but I got no business there.” “ If she’s the one got married you shore as the devil got no business there.” “I got tired o’ him traipsin’ off on the sly to see that gal.” A married man stops at the house of a woman he courted long ago (slight flirting). Mentions a bear’s breast. Describes a man taking off his long underwear. A married man makes a joke about a woman he used to court: “Spent the night with an old gal o’ mine...Only she wasn’t there!” (purposeful insinuation) A song’s lines are given and include a woman falling in love, a man marrying another woman and wishing he was single. The word “breasts” is used to mean chests. A boy feels possessive of a girl “if only to throw potatoes at her.” The town gathers for a Christmas dance. An woman kisses a boy on the forehead. He likes it.<br /><br />Conversation Topics - 5 Incidents: One family is known for their drinking and getting into drunken brawls. They mention several times how much they like alcohol - it is spoken of positively several times. The town doctor is a drunk. This is throughout the book. Tobacco (t’baccy) and pipes are mentioned throughout the book. A child drinks some wine (he wishes it was sweeter). A joke is told and includes how a doctor can sometimes fool God. A woman talks about why she doesn’t want to go to heaven.<br /><br />**Like my reviews? Then you should follow me! Because I have hundreds more just like this one. With each review, I provide a Cleanliness Report, mentioning any objectionable content I come across so that parents and/or conscientious readers (like me) can determine beforehand whether they want to read a book or not. Content surprises are super annoying, especially when you’re 100+ pages in, so here’s my attempt to help you avoid that! <br /><br />So Follow or Friend me here on GoodReads! You’ll see my updates as I’m reading and know which books I’m liking and what I’m not finishing and why. You’ll also be able to utilize my library for looking up titles to see whether the book you’re thinking about reading next has any objectionable content or not. From swear words, to romance, to bad attitudes (in children’s books), I cover it all!<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4123245184124509193" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('ZPlLjjueQzcKDuUsxMcOcZZ15mqrohJqP3EU8xk/LrkUTEFDX8F6t/v69saqM29cQ2qhVbf5IjQSlGdVi8v5CQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('YQcGqc9I+36HTFjx0ZUyMtuVoF+skkIPxYUZm3Pw6nwRsgxkqxfC/na4Sxu/YVMfDornYLDJclHoYGo94QQ9zA==')})
  }

The novel moves through a single year and, at the same time, a decade of years, from the spring arrival of the family at their mortgaged farm to the winter 10 years later, when the ravages of drought, fire, and personal anguish have led to the deaths of two of the five. Like Ethan Frome, the relatively brief, intense story evokes the torment possible among people isolated and driven by strong feelings of love and hate that, unexpressed, lead inevitably to doom. Reviewers in the thirties praised the novel, calling its prose &quot;profoundly moving music,&quot; expressing incredulity &quot;that this mature style and this mature point of view are those of a It was the first novel by a Georgia author to win a Pulitzer, soon followed by Margaret Mitchell\'s Gone With the Wind in 1937. In fact, Lamb was largely responsible for the discovery of Gone With the Wind; after reading Miller\'s novel, Macmillan editor Harold S. Latham sought other southern novels and authors, and found Margaret Mitchell.\n\nCaroline Miller was fascinated by the other Old South—not the romantic inhabitants of Gone With the Wind, but rather the poor people of the south Georgia backwoods, who never owned a slave or planned to fight a war. The story of Cean and Lonzo, a young couple who begin their married lives two decades before the Civil War, Lamb in His Bosom is a fascinating account of social customs and material realities among settlers of the Georgia frontier. At the same time, Lamb in His Bosom transcends regional history as Miller\'s quietly lyrical prose style pays poignant tribute to a woman\'s life lived close to nature—the nature outside her and the The novel\'s action begins in 1884, when Grover Cleveland became the first Democra<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText10550261518266590644\" style=\"display:none\">Winner of a Pulitzer Prize in 1933, The Store is the second novel of Stribling\'s monumental trilogy set in the author\'s native Tennessee Valley region of North Alabama. The novel\'s action begins in 1884, when Grover Cleveland became the first Democratic president since the end of the Civil War, and it centers about the emergence of Colonel Miltiades Vaiden as a figure of wealth and power in the city of Florence. \nIn The Store, Stribling succeeds in presenting the essence of an age through the everyday lives of his characters. <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer16586875068304465567">Marjorie Kinnan Rawlings was an American author who lived in rural Florida and wrote novels with rural themes and settings. Her best known work, The Yearling, about a boy who adopts an orphaned fawn, won a Pulitzer Prize for fiction in 1939 and was later made into a movie of the same title, <i>The Yearling</i>. The book was written long before the concept of young-adult fiction, but is now commonly inclu</span>
  <span id="freeText16586875068304465567" style="display:none">Marjorie Kinnan Rawlings was an American author who lived in rural Florida and wrote novels with rural themes and settings. Her best known work, The Yearling, about a boy who adopts an orphaned fawn, won a Pulitzer Prize for fiction in 1939 and was later made into a movie of the same title, <i>The Yearling</i>. The book was written long before the concept of young-adult fiction, but is now commonly included in teen-reading lists.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16586875068304465567" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/583534-the-yearling">Quotes from The Yearling</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">

            <nobr>

          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;You've seed how things goes in the world o' men. You've knowed men to be low-down and mean. You've seed ol' Death at his tricks...Ever' man wants life to be a fine thing, and a easy. 'Tis fine, boy, powerful fine, but 'tain't easy. Life knocks a man down and he gits up and it knocks him down agin. I've been uneasy all my life...I've wanted life to be easy for you. Easier'n 'twas for me. A man's heart aches, seein' his young uns face the world. Knowin' they got to get their guts tore out, the way his was tore. I wanted to spare you, long as I could. I wanted you to frolic with your yearlin'. I knowed the lonesomeness he eased for you. But ever' man's lonesome. What's he to do then? What's he to do when he gits knocked down? Why, take it for his share and go on. <br /><br /> —Penny Baxter&rdquo;</span>
